        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.7b16402"><meta property="og:title" content="CNT-Decorated Na3V2(PO4)3 Microspheres as a High-Rate and Cycle-Stable Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/acsami.7b16402.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="A novel cathode material, carbon nanotube (CNT)-decorated Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) microspheres, was designed and synthesized via spray-drying and carbothermal reduction methods. The microspheres were covered and embedded by CNTs, the surfaces of which were also covered by amorphous carbon layers. Thus, a carbon network composed of CNTs and amorphous carbon layers formed in the materials. The polarization of a 10 wt % CNT-decorated NVP (NVP/C10) electrode was much less compared with that of the electrode with pristine NVP without CNTs. The capacity of the NVP/C10 electrode only decreased from 103.2 to 76.2 mAh g–1 when the current rates increased from 0.2 to 60 C. Even when cycled at a rate of 20 C, the initial discharge capacity of the NVP/C10 electrode was as high as 91.2 mAh g–1, and the discharge capacity was 76.9 mAh g–1 after 150 cycles. The charge-transfer resistance and ohmic resistance became smaller because of CNT decorating. Meanwhile, the addition of CNTs can tune the size of the NVP particles and increase the contact area between NVP and the electrolyte. Consequently, the resulted NVP had a larger sodium ion diffusion coefficient than that of the pristine NVP."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/acsami.7b16402.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931887ddac8454b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931887ddac8454b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '39f1e03c-e1a6-4003-a34f-17c9bc15849e'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931887ddac8454b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="931887ddac8454b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CNT-Decorated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Microspheres as a High-Rate and Cycle-Stable Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b16402", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CNT-Decorated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Microspheres as a High-Rate and Cycle-Stable Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b16402", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CNT-Decorated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Microspheres as a High-Rate and Cycle-Stable Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b16402", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CNT-Decorated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Microspheres as a High-Rate and Cycle-Stable Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b16402", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CNT-Decorated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Microspheres as a High-Rate and Cycle-Stable Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b16402", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CNT-Decorated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Microspheres as a High-Rate and Cycle-Stable Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b16402", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CNT-Decorated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Microspheres as a High-Rate and Cycle-Stable Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b16402", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CNT-Decorated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Microspheres as a High-Rate and Cycle-Stable Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b16402", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CNT-Decorated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Microspheres as a High-Rate and Cycle-Stable Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b16402", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">108</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/medium/am-2017-16402x_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b16402&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">A novel cathode material, carbon nanotube (CNT)-decorated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) microspheres, was designed and synthesized via spray-drying and carbothermal reduction methods. The microspheres were covered and embedded by CNTs, the surfaces of which were also covered by amorphous carbon layers. Thus, a carbon network composed of CNTs and amorphous carbon layers formed in the materials. The polarization of a 10 wt % CNT-decorated NVP (NVP/C10) electrode was much less compared with that of the electrode with pristine NVP without CNTs. The capacity of the NVP/C10 electrode only decreased from 103.2 to 76.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when the current rates increased from 0.2 to 60 C. Even when cycled at a rate of 20 C, the initial discharge capacity of the NVP/C10 electrode was as high as 91.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and the discharge capacity was 76.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 150 cycles. The charge-transfer resistance and ohmic resistance became smaller because of CNT decorating. Meanwhile, the addition of CNTs can tune the size of the NVP particles and increase the contact area between NVP and the electrolyte. Consequently, the resulted NVP had a larger sodium ion diffusion coefficient than that of the pristine NVP.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D3590%26pageCount%3D6%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DHezhang%2BChen%252C%2BBao%2BZhang%252C%2BXu%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D4%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b16402%26title%3DCNT-Decorated%2BNa3V2%2528PO4%25293%2BMicrospheres%2Bas%2Ba%2BHigh-Rate%2Band%2BCycle-Stable%2BCathode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D6%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D3595%26publicationDate%3DJanuary%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291646&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon nanotubes"> Carbon nanotubes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials"> Materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292123&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Microspheres"> Microspheres </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na3v23&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na3V2%28PO4%293">Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Microspheres&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_microspheres">microspheres</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Nanotubes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbon%20nanotubes">carbon nanotubes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Amorphous+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_amorphous%20carbon">amorphous carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The scarcity of fossil fuel and the problem of environmental pollution drive people to develop new energy resources. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1-5)</a> Lithium ion batteries have become an important part in our lives in the past two decades. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 to 8">(6-8)</a> However, lithium resources cannot satisfy increasing market demands. So, searching for alternative sources is vital to the development of batteries. Sodium possesses similar physical and chemical properties to lithium and is richly abundant on the earth. Sodium ion battery materials have become a hot research topic in the energy domain, as sodium ion batteries have promise to replace lithium ion batteries in future. It is necessary to develop a sodium ion battery with excellent electrochemical performance to meet the requirement of energy storage.</div><div class="NLM_p">As the main part of battery, the cathode material is the restraining factor in the improvement of properties. The properties, such as the battery safety, cost, energy density, cycle life length, and so on, mainly depend on the cathode material. Lots of cathode materials such as Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> Na<sub>0.85</sub>Li<sub>0.17</sub>Ni<sub>0.21</sub>Mn<sub>0.64</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>(Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>)O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> and Prussian blue analogues <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> were investigated for sodium ion batteries. Among these materials, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) is regarded as one of the most important battery materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> NVP owns a NASICON framework structure and possesses many good merits, such as excellent thermal stability and high energy density. However, like other phosphate salt electrode materials, NVP has poor electronic conductivity. Furthermore, the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> is more difficult than that of Li<sup>+</sup>, as the radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) is larger than that of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å). Therefore, many attempts have been made to enhance the electrochemical properties, such as diminishing particle size and using carbon coating or cation doping. Cation doping could increase the electronic conductivity of NVP due to the generation of local defects in the structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16, 17)</a> Decreasing the size of particles can shorten the diffusion path of sodium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 18 to 20">(18-20)</a> Carbon coating is an efficient way to improve the electronic conductivity of cathode materials. The amorphous carbon was coated on the NVP particle surface by using sucrose as the carbon source, and the obtained cathode exhibited good wide-temperature performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Besides amorphous carbon, carbon nanotubes (CNTs) were also used as a conductive network to coat the electrode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 22 to 25">(22-25)</a> CNTs have a high degree of graphitized carbon, which is helpful for enhancing the electronic conductivity of energy materials. In this work, CNT-decorated NVP (NVP/CNT) microsphere cathode material with a novel structure and excellent performance was designed and synthesized. CNTs and amorphous carbon were both used to modify NVP through spray drying and carbothermal reduction. The NVP microspheres were embedded and covered by CNTs, and the particles were coated by amorphous carbon layers. Hence, a carbon network formed in the cathode material. Furthermore, NVP microspheres were separated by CNTs, which is beneficial for decreasing the size of primary particles and increasing the contact area between NVP and the electrolyte. In addition to the improvement of electronic conductivity, the amorphous carbon layers can protect NVP particles against erosion from the electrolyte. As a result, the electrochemical properties of the NVP/CNTs were improved remarkably. The influences of CNTs on the crystal structure as well as the reason for enhancing electrochemical properties are explored and discussed in detail.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">CNTs were purchased from Chengdu Organic Chemicals Co., Ltd., China. The CNTs were dispersed in the solution containing HNO<sub>3</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> (1:3 in volume) at 80 °C for 5 h. The acid-treated CNTs were washed by distilled water and then freeze dried. NVP/CNT microspheres were synthesized by spray-drying and carbothermal reduction methods. First, citric acid and NaHCO<sub>3</sub> were dissolved in deionized water by continuous stirring. Second, NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> and NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> were dissolved in the above solution and heated to 80 °C (molar ratio of citric acid to NaHCO<sub>3</sub> to NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> to NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> was 6:3:2:3). After obtaining a blue solution, the acid-treated CNTs were added into the solution under ultrasonic treatment for about 5 h. The homogeneous slurry was spray dried at 150 °C to get a powder sample. The spray-dried powder was sintered at 800 °C for 10 h under an Ar atmosphere. Finally, the NVP/CNT samples were obtained, in which the CNT amounts were 5, 10, and 15 wt %. The NVP/CNT samples with different amounts of CNTs were labeled as NVP/C5 (5 wt % CNTs), NVP/C10 (10 wt % CNTs), and NVP/C15 (15 wt % CNTs), respectively. The NVP sample without CNTs was prepared by the same way, without adding CNTs in the process, labeled as NVP/C.</div><div class="NLM_p">The phases of all of the samples were investigated by X-ray diffraction (XRD, Rigaku D/Max 200PC, Japan) using Cu Kα radiation. The scanning rate was 5° min<sup>–1</sup>, and the scanning range of diffraction angle (2θ) was 10–60°. The morphologies of all of the samples were observed by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Nova NanoSEM-230, U.S.A.) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM, FEI Tecnai G2 F20 S-Twin, U.S.A., working at 200 kV). The Raman spectra were studied by a laser Raman spectroscope (Raman, HORIBA JOBIN YVON, France).</div><div class="NLM_p last">The cathode material was evaluated using 2025-type coin cells prepared in an argon-filled glovebox. The slurry obtained by mixing the prepared powders, carbon black, and polyvinylidene fluoride (8:1:1 in weight) in <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidinone was pasted onto aluminum foil. Then, the foil was dried at 120 °C for 4 h in air. The electrolyte was a 1 M solution of NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbon/dimethyl carbonate (1:1 w/w) and 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate. The counter electrode was a sodium foil, and a glass microfiber (Whatman GF/D) was used as a separator. Electrochemical tests were carried out using an automatic galvanostatic charge–discharge unit, a LAND battery cycler, with the potential of 2.0–3.8 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at room temperature. The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were conducted by an electrochemical workstation (CHI 660D, CH Instruments, China). The amplitude voltage was 5 mV, and the frequency range was 10<sup>–2</sup>–10<sup>5</sup> Hz. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was also conducted by this workstation, with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">NVP/CNT microspheres were synthesized by spray-drying and carbothermal reduction methods. To explore the influence of CNTs on the electrochemical properties of NVP material, NVP/CNT samples with different amounts of CNTs were synthesized in this work. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> shows the XRD patterns of the samples with different amounts of CNTs, which are all consistent well with the R3¯m space group of the rhombohedral NASICON structure. There are two visible weak impurity peaks around 26 and 35° in the patterns of the NVP/C10 and NVP/C15 samples, corresponding to the characteristic peaks of CNTs. It is found that the crystal structures of the samples were not changed by CNT embedding. To determine the amount of carbon present, a carbon–sulfur analyzer was applied to study the samples. The measured carbon amounts of NVP/C, NVP/C5, NVP/C10, and NVP/C15 were 1.00, 4.66, 6.42, and 9.52 wt %, respectively. The carbon in the NVP/C sample without CNTs originates from the carbothermal reduction of citric acid.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/medium/am-2017-16402x_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. XRD patterns of NVP/C and NVP/CNT samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b16402&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the characteristics of the carbon layers of all of the NVP samples, Raman spectroscopy was applied to study the samples, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. The patterns show that there are two broadened D bands and a G band for all of the samples, which represent disordered carbon (sp<sup>3</sup>-coordinated behavior) and crystalline graphitized carbon (sp<sup>2</sup>-coordinated behavior), respectively. The relative intensity ratio of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> indicates the degree of structural disorder of the carbon materials. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratios of NVP/C, NVP/C5, NVP/C10, and NVP/C15 samples were 0.90, 0.88, 0.85, and 0.81, respectively. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio of the NVP/C15 sample was the lowest, suggesting the CNTs in it were highly graphitized; the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio of NVP was the highest, as the carbon layer is not completely graphitized. So, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratios of the samples decreased with the increase in CNT content.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/medium/am-2017-16402x_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Raman spectra of CNT, NVP/C, and NVP/CNT samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b16402&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphologies of all samples were observed by SEM, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. It is found that all the sample particles were spherical, with a size of 2–3 μm. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a shows the SEM image of the NVP/C (without CNT) microsphere. It is seen that some large primary particles are located on the surface of the microsphere. However, when the CNT content was 5 wt %, the large primary particles on the surface of the microsphere became smaller; when the CNT content was increased to 10 and 15 wt %, the primary particles on the surface disappeared, as the growth of the primary particles was controlled by the CNTs. Furthermore, the microspheres were covered and embedded by the CNTs, which is expected to enhance the electrochemical properties of the NVP cathode. TEM and HRTEM were carried out to further observe the NVP/CNT microspheres, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the TEM image of the microspheres at low magnification, and the TEM image is consistent with the SEM result. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, it can be seen that CNTs are embedded in the NVP microsphere and the carbon network has formed, which could significantly improve the conductivity of the NVP materials. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, HRTEM shows an obvious crystal lattice spacing of about 0.375 nm, which matches well with the (113) plane of NVP. Furthermore, one CNT is found to be embedded into the inside of the particle. Meanwhile, it is seen that a thin amorphous carbon layer covered the microsphere, the thickness of which was about 1 nm. The amorphous carbon is beneficial in protecting the surface of the cathode material from suffering corrosion from the electrolyte. The carbon network composed of an amorphous carbon layer, and CNTs can provide a better interfacial contact among NVP particles and can accelerate the electron transport among the particles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Meanwhile, the electrolyte is able to easily penetrate into the inside of the microspheres, as the microspheres were porous due to the embedding of the CNTs, increasing the contact area between the electrolyte and NVP particle surface.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/medium/am-2017-16402x_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. SEM images of (a) NVP/C, (b) NVP/C5, (c) NVP/C10, and (d) NVP/C15 samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b16402&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/medium/am-2017-16402x_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a,b) TEM and (c) HRTEM images of NVP/C10 sample.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b16402&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical properties of all electrodes were studied in half cells, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the charging and discharging curves of all of the electrodes, and the charge and discharge rates were 0.2 and 1 C (1 C = 110 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), respectively. The discharge capacities of NVP/C, NVP/C5, NVP/C10, and NVP/C15 were 103.6, 101.5, 100.3, and 97.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, which decreased with the increase in CNT contents. The reason is that the CNTs contribute nothing to the discharge capacity. The rate performance of the electrodes was shown in the inset in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. It is seen that the rate performance of NVP was improved by the embedding of the CNTs. Among the NVP/CNT electrodes, the NVP/C10 electrode exhibited the best electrochemical property. For investigating the Na ion insertion/extraction behavior, CV of NVP/C and NVP/C10 electrodes was conducted, as displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b There is only one redox couple in the curves, located at around 3.4 V, assigned to the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>–NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) redox couple. The sharp peaks of the NVP/C10 electrode indicating the transformation between the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase and NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase is more reversible. The potential intervals between the cathodic and anodic peaks of the NVP/C and NVP/C10 electrodes were 0.260 and 0.177 V, respectively. It suggests that the polarization of the NVP/C10 electrode is less than that of the NVP/C electrode, as CNTs can enhance the ion and electron conduction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref26" aria-label="reference 19 and 26">(19, 26)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/medium/am-2017-16402x_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Charging and discharging curves of the electrodes; the inset is the rate performance of the electrodes; (b) CV of NVP/C and NVP/C10 electrodes; charging and discharging curves of (c) NVP/C and (d) NVP/C10 electrodes at various current rates; (e) rate performance and (f) cycle performance of NVP/C and NVP/C10 electrodes at various current rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b16402&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c,d shows the charging and discharging curves of the NVP/C and NVP/C10 electrodes at different C rates, respectively, in which the charge rate was kept at 0.2 C. The NVP/C electrodes delivered capacities of 113.0, 101.5, 101, 96.7, 92.8, 87.7, 82.8, 73.4, and 61.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2, 1, 5, 10, 15, 20, 25, and 30 C, respectively, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c. For NVP/C10 electrodes, the discharge capacities of which at 0.2, 1, 5, 20, 30, and 40 C were 103.2, 100.3, 97.4, 94.1, 91.2, and 88.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d. In particular, when the discharge rate was increased to 50 and 60 C, the discharge capacities of NVP/C10 still achieved 84.3 and 76.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Apparently, the discharge voltage plateaus of the NVP/C electrode were lower than those of the NVP/C10 electrodes at different current rates. It is further confirmed that the polarization of NVP was reduced by the CNTs, which is in agreement with the CV results. Meanwhile, the NVP/C10 electrodes exhibited much higher discharge capacities compared with those of the NVP/C electrodes at high rates. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e shows the rate performances of NVP/C and NVP/C10 electrodes. It is seen that the initial discharge capacity of NVP/C10 was about 10 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> lower than that of NVP/C at 0.2 C, as the CNTs do not involve the electrochemical reaction. However, when the rate was increased to 5 C, the discharge capacities of the NVP/C10 electrodes exceeded those of NVP/C electrodes. With the increase in current rates, the discharge capacities of the NVP/C10 electrodes decreased more slowly compared with those of the NVP/C electrodes. Especially, when the rate was increased to 60 C, the capacity retention of the NVP/C10 electrode was 73.8%; when the rate decreased to 0.2 C, the discharge capacity reached 102.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which is close to the initial capacity. This indicates that the insertion/extraction of the sodium ion in the NVP/C10 electrode was more reversible even at high rates. Cycle performance is also an important factor for evaluating cathode materials. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f shows the cycle performance of NVP/C and NVP/C10 electrodes at 10 and 20 C. It can be easily observed that the cycle performance of the NVP/C10 electrode was much better than that of NVP/C electrode. The initial discharge capacities of the NVP/C10 electrode were 96.0 and 91.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 and 20 C, respectively. After 150 cycles, the discharge capacities were 88.9 and 76.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and the capacity retention values were 92.60 and 84.32% at 10 and 20 C, respectively. The CNT network can accelerate the electron transport and make the NVP microsphere become porous, which can shorten the sodium diffusion path. Therefore, the electrochemical performance, including rate and cycle performances, of CNT-decorated NVP cathode materials was greatly improved.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to investigate the effect of CNTs on the electrochemical properties and electrode reaction kinetics of the electrodes, EIS was employed to investigate the NVP/C and NVP/C10 electrodes. The Nyquist plots of the electrodes before cycling are displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a. A semicircle and a straight line were found in each of the Nyquist plots for both electrodes. The intercept in the high-frequency region corresponds to the ohmic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>) of the electrolyte and electrode. The semicircle in the high–medium frequency is related to the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), which represents the diffusion of the sodium ion between the electrode and electrolyte. The inclined line at low frequency represents the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>ω</sub>), which is associated with sodium ion diffusion in the NVP particles. The inset of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>ais a simplified equivalent circuit model, which is constructed to analyze the impedance spectra. A constant phase element (CPE) represents the double layer capacitance and passivation film capacitance. The impedance parameters obtained from the equivalent circuit are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>. Notably, the NVP/C10 electrode exhibited a much smaller <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> compared with those of the NVP/C electrode. It means that the CNTs obviously decreased <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, which further confirms that CNTs could enhance the rate performance of the samples. Furthermore, the diffusion coefficient of sodium ions (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) can be calculated from the plots, and the equation can be expressed as follows <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 27 to 30">(27-30)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/am-2017-16402x_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/am-2017-16402x_m002.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></span>where <i>R</i> is the gas constant, <i>T</i> denotes the absolute temperature, <i>A</i> denotes the surface area of the cathode, <i>n</i> signifies the number of transferred electrons, <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant, <i>C</i> is the concentration of sodium ions, σ is the Warburg coefficient, and ω is the angular frequency in the low-frequency region. The linear fitting of <i>Z</i>′ versus ω<sup><i>–1/2</i></sup> is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b, in which the value of σ is the slope.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/medium/am-2017-16402x_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Nyquist plots of NVP/C and NVP/C10 electrodes; the inset is the equivalent circuit for EIS result fitting; (b) the relationship between <i>Z</i>′ and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> in the low-frequency region.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b16402/asset/images/large/am-2017-16402x_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b16402&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Impedance Parameters of NVP/C and NVP/C10 Electrodes Obtained from Equivalent Circuit Fitting</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table" border="0"><colgroup><col align="center"><col align="center"><col align="center"><col align="center"><col align="center"></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">sample</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center"><i>R</i><sub>s</sub></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center"><i>R</i><sub>ct</sub></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">σ</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center"><i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>&nbsp;(cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>–1</sup>)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="center">NVP/C</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">28.2</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">589.6</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">56.8</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">9.14&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>–14</sup></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="center">NVP/C10</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">15.8</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">225.3</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">30.8</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">3.11&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>–13</sup></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p last"><i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> was calculated according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eqs1">eqs</a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>, and the obtained values are listed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>. The <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of the NVP/C10 electrode was 3.11 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, which is much higher than that of the NVP/C electrode (9.14 × 10<sup>–14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>). This demonstrates that the <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> in the charge and discharge processes was obviously increased by CNT addition. The lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> and higher <i>D</i><sub><i>Na+</i></sub> are beneficial in improving the electrochemical properties of the electrode.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, CNT-decorated NVP microspheres were prepared by spray-drying and carbothermal reduction methods. It has been found that the microspheres were embedded by CNTs and covered by amorphous carbon layers. So, the carbon network formed in the materials, which could accelerate the electron transport among the NVP particles. And the NVP particles were separated by CNTs, which shorten the sodium ion diffusion path. As a result, the NVP/CNT composites exhibited a significant enhancement in electrochemical properties, including the rate and cycle performances. The NVP/C10 electrode exhibited a discharge capacity of 76.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 60 C. The capacity retentions of the NVP/C10 electrode were 92.60 and 84.32% at 10 and 20 C, after 150 cycles, respectively. The excellent electrochemical performance can be attributed to the novel spherical structure, composed of NVP, CNTs, and amorphous carbon layers. Therefore, the synthesized CNT-decorated NVP microspheres can be considered as a promising cathode material for sodium ion batteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="_suppInfo"></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b16402" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="authorInformationSection"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="authorInformationSection">Author Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><ul><li><span class="author-information-subsection-header">Corresponding Author</span><ul><li><div class="authorItemInformation"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor strong">Hui Tong</span> - <span class="hlFld-Affiliation affiliation">School of Metallurgy and Environment, Central South University, Changsha, 410083, P.R.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Quantitative Structure-Activity Relationship (QSAR) for the Oxidation of Trace Organic Contaminants by Sulfate Radical</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Ruiyang; Ye, Tiantian; Wei, Zongsu; Luo, Shuang; Yang, Zhihui; Spinney, Richard</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Environmental Science &amp; Technology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">22</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">13394-13402</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ESTHAG</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-936X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The sulfate radical anion (SO4•-) based oxidn. of trace org. contaminants (TrOCs) has recently received great attention due to its high reactivity and low selectivity.  In this study, a meta-anal. was conducted to better understand the role of functional groups on the reactivity between SO4•- and TrOCs.  The results indicate that compds. in which electron transfer and addn. channels dominate tend to exhibit a faster second-order rate consts. (kSO•-4) than that of H-atom abstraction, corroborating the SO4•- reactivity and mechanisms obsd. in the individual studies.  Then, a quant. structure activity relationship (QSAR) model was developed using a sequential approach with constitutional, geometrical, electrostatic, and quantum chem. descriptors.  Two descriptors, ELUMO and EHOMO energy gap (ELUMO-EHOMO) and the ratio of oxygen atoms to carbon atoms (#O:C), were found to mechanistically and statistically affect kSO•-4 to a great extent with the standardized QSAR model: ln kSO•-4 = 26.8-3.97 × #O:C - 0.746 × (ELUMO-EHOMO).  In addn., the correlation anal. indicates that there is no dominant reaction channel for SO4•- reactions with various structurally diverse compds.  Our QSAR model provides a robust predictive tool for estg. emerging micropollutants removal using SO4•- during wastewater treatment processes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1021/acs.est.5b03744"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zammit, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wei, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, W. P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">MacLeod, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Spinney, R.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Kinetics and Mechanism of the Oxidation of Cyclic Methylsiloxanes by Hydroxyl Radical in the Gas Phase: An Experimental and Theoretical Study</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Environ. Sci. Technol.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">49</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">13322</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">13330</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.est.5b03744</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D49%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D13322-13330%26journal%3DEnviron.%2BSci.%2BTechnol.%26author%3DR.%2BXiao%26author%3DI.%2BZammit%26author%3DZ.%2BWei%26author%3DW.%2BP.%2BHu%26author%3DM.%2BMacLeod%26author%3DR.%2BSpinney%26title%3DKinetics%2Band%2BMechanism%2Bof%2Bthe%2BOxidation%2Bof%2BCyclic%2BMethylsiloxanes%2Bby%2BHydroxyl%2BRadical%2Bin%2Bthe%2BGas%2BPhase%253A%2BAn%2BExperimental%2Band%2BTheoretical%2BStudy%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.est.5b03744&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b16402&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.est.5b03744&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Kinetics and Mechanism of the Oxidation of Cyclic Methylsiloxanes by Hydroxyl Radical in the Gas Phase: An Experimental and Theoretical Study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Ruiyang; Zammit, Ian; Wei, Zongsu; Hu, Wei-Ping; MacLeod, Matthew; Spinney, Richard</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Environmental Science &amp; Technology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">22</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">13322-13330</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The ubiquitous presence of cyclic volatile methylsiloxanes (cVMS) in the global atm. has recently raised environmental concern.  In order to assess the persistence and long-range transport potential of cVMS, their second-order rate consts. (k) for reactions with hydroxyl radical (•OH) in the gas phase are needed.  We exptl. and theor. investigated the kinetics and mechanism of •OH oxidn. of a series of cVMS, hexamethylcyclotrisiloxane (D3), octamethylcyclotetrasiloxane (D4), and decamethycyclopentasiloxane (D5). We also calcd. the thermodn. and kinetic behaviors for reactions at the M06-2X/6-311++G**//M06-2X/6-31+G** level of theory over a wider temp. range of 238-358 K that encompasses temps. in the troposphere. The measured k values were approx. an order of magnitude higher than the theor. values but have the same trend with increasing size of the siloxane ring.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The second-order rate consts. (k) of hydroxyl radical (·OH) with polychlorinated biphenyls (PCBs) in the gas phase are of scientific and regulatory importance for assessing their global distribution and fate in the atm.  Due to the limited no. of measured k values, there is a need to model the k values for unknown PCBs congeners.  In the present study, we developed a quant. structure-activity relationship (QSAR) model with quantum chem. descriptors using a sequential approach, including correlation anal., principal component anal., multi-linear regression, validation, and estn. of applicability domain. The cryst. phase, chem. valence state and the microstructure of Li3VO4-coated material were investigated by x-ray diffraction, energy dispersive spectroscopy, XPS and transmission electron microscopy.  The existence of the Li3VO4 coating layer on the surface of sphere particle was validated, and the electrochem. performances of the samples were studied. Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy and cyclic voltammetry performance analyses revealed that the excellent electrochem. performance of the Li3VO4-coated sample is ascribed to the improved Li ion cond. and the suppression of the chem. reaction between the electrode material and electrolyte.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1038/nmat2920"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Berthelot, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Carlier, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Delmas, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical investigation of the P2-NaxCoO<sub>2</sub> phase diagram</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical investigation of the P2-NaxCoO2 phase diagram</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Berthelot, R.; Carlier, D.; Delmas, C.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na layered oxides NaxCoO2 form one of the most fascinating low-dimensional and strongly correlated systems; in particular P2-NaxCoO2 exhibits various single-phase domains with different Na+/vacancy patterns depending on the Na concn.  Here the authors used Na batteries to clearly depict the P2-NaxCoO2 phase diagram for x ≥ 0.50.  By coupling the electrochem. process with an in situ x-ray diffraction expt., the authors identified the succession of single-phase or two-phase domains appearing on Na intercalation with a rather good accuracy compared with previous studies.  The authors reported new single-phase domains and the authors underlined the thermal instability of some ordered phases from an electrochem. study at various temps.  As each phase was characterized by the position of its Fermi level vs. the Na+/Na couple, the synthesis of each material, even in large amts., can be carried out electrochem.  The phys. properties of the as-prepd. Na1/2CoO2 and Na2/3CoO2 ordered phases were characterized and compared.  Electrochem. processes are an accurate route to precisely study in a continuous way such a complex system and provide a new way to synthesize materials with a very narrow existence range.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1021/ja509704t"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Billaud, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Clement, R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">There is much interest in Na-ion batteries for grid storage because of the lower projected cost compared with Li-ion. Here such a material, β-NaMnO2, is investigated with a different structure from that of NaMnO2 polymorphs and other compds. studied extensively in the past.  It exhibits a high capacity (of ca. 190 mA h g-1 at a rate of C/20), along with a good rate capability (142 mA h g-1 at a rate of 2C) and a good capacity retention (100 mA h g-1after 100 Na extn./insertion cycles at a rate of 2C).                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Improved Electrochemical Performance of Fe-Substituted NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yuan, Ding D.; Wang, Yan X.; Cao, Yu L.; Ai, Xin P.; Yang, Han X.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
